  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_b7932e89-0257-4797-94d9-a8ad425294a7'
      });

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer1509384975609924242">Unter Hoffmanns Werken hat Der Sandmann das weitaus größte Interesse gefunden. Sein Grundproblem ist die Selbstverfallenheit des Menschen, der aus dem Gefängnis seines Ichs nicht mehr herausfindet, deshalb in seinen Lebensbeziehungen, vor allem in seiner Liebesbindung tragisch scheitert und am Ende sich selbst zerstören muß.</span>

      $('rating_details_tip').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_3351415894" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3351415894" />
    <a title="Greta" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/111566858-greta"><img alt="Greta" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586844759p2/111566858.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3351415894?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greta" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greta" href="/user/show/111566858-greta">Greta</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/29447650-the-sandman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/111566858-greta?shelf=best-reviews">best-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/111566858-greta?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3351415894" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17925628860564648971"><b>The Classic that first described Schizophrenia</b><br/><br/>E.T.A Hoffmann was way before his time, when he wrote "The Sandmann" in 1816. He describes the development of schizophrenia to its worst consequence, at a time where it wasn’t recognized as a psychological disorder yet. Only after Sigmund Freud used this novel in his thesis 100 years later to show the exemplary development of such a disease, it became a masterpiece of analysis. <br/><br/><b>Nathanael</b><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1605872198i/30403151.jpg' width='500' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><i>Nathanael writes about his severe childhood trauma</i><br/><br/>As a child Nat</span>
  <span id="freeText17925628860564648971" style="display:none"><b>The Classic that first described Schizophrenia</b><br><br>E.T.A Hoffmann was way before his time, when he wrote "The Sandmann" in 1816. He describes the development of schizophrenia to its worst consequence, at a time where it wasn’t recognized as a psychological disorder yet. Only after Sigmund Freud used this novel in his thesis 100 years later to show the exemplary development of such a disease, it became a masterpiece of analysis. <br><br><b>Nathanael</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1605872198i/30403151.jpg" width="500" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><i>Nathanael writes about his severe childhood trauma</i><br><br>As a child Nathanael was obsessively afraid of "The Sandman", though he wasn't sure whether he was afraid of a man or monster. In his childhood the tale of the mysterious Sandman terrorized him, who was said to throw sand in the eyes of children to steal their eyes and feed them to his own children, if they wouldn‘t go to bed. Nathanael associated the Sandman with a man who visited his father regularly at 9pm, which is why he and his siblings had to be in bed by then. One night he hid in his father's room to see the Sandman and discovered that it was Coppelius, an obnoxious lawyer, who came to carry out alchemical experiments. When Nathanael saw that he took "shining masses" out of the fire and hammered them into face-like shapes without eyes, he screamed and was discovered. Coppelius pushed him to the fireplace and tried to throw embers into Nathanael's eyes. Because his father pleaded that he could keep his eyes, Coppelius twisted Nathanael's hands and feet and tortured him until he passed out instead. One year later his father died due to a kind of flaming explosion, in another night of experiments with Coppelius, who then vanished without a trace. The burns to his father‘s face are gone before he is laid in his coffin. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1605872431i/30403188.jpg" width="500" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><i>Nathanael thinks that the man who calls himself Coppola and turns up at his door, is in fact the Sandman in disguise</i><br><br>Nathanael believes that a barometer-seller who just arrived at his door and calls himself Coppola is in fact the Sandman from his childhood in disguise. These characters merge into one another throughout the story, and it remains unclear whether or not they are the same person. Then Nathanael falls in love with a strange woman named Olimpia, even though he is engaged to Clara. But he ultimately realizes that Olimpia is a lifelike wooden doll full of machinery, created by Professor Spalanzani and Coppola, who obsessively focuses on her eyes, which is why it can be guessed that the characters are truly one and the same. Just when it seems that Nathanael is able to free himself from his insanity, his hallucinations push himself to suicide.<br><br><b> Three Letters</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1605872302i/30403154.jpg" width="500" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><i>The story is told through letters, written and exchanged between him, his fiancé Clara and her brother Lothar </i><br><br>The story evolves around Nathanael but is told from different perspectives through letters, exchanged between him, his fiancé Clara and her brother Lothar. Using that tool, the distinction between ill thoughts, experience, memory and hallucinations is blurry from the very beginning. By telling the story of the disturbed mind instead of explaining it, the reader is confronted with the raw fear and uncertainty of experiences, you can’t tell to be true or not. <br><br><b>Eyes and Schizophrenia</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1605872794i/30403250.jpg" width="500" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><i>The use and exposure of mental illness is the basis for creating a lack of trust in one's own or another's experienced sensation and perception.<br></i><br>The story works a lot with eyes, as symbols for the entry to our soul and the feeling of being watched. Due to their severe violation in the storyline they become a key point for a disease eating deep in his soul and setting off his hallucinations that people are not who they seem to be. The reader constantly has to assess Nathanael's level of insanity to decide what to believe of the story, which transports a sense of suddenness and overwhelming madness. Nathanael shows a great affliction to surveillance and machines, both attributes that are extremely common elements of schizophrenic hallucinations. Pain, fire and heat symbolize the progression of his disease and the feeling of a psychotic episode. It might also stand for a high fever caused by a childhood disease, responsible for fever induced hallucinations, warped perception, and true perception of a crazy situation. It’s a childhood trauma abducting rationality till death.<br><br><i>“It is the phantom of our own self whose deep affinity and profound influence on our state of mind either damns us to hell or uplifts us into heaven.” </i><br><br>This book can not be read without an analytical eye, or you won’t be able to understand it, but if you do it it eats you up!</span>
  <a data-text-id="17925628860564648971" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10349982742618000375">Brilliant book starting with a kid's fear of the sandman. Problem is the 'sandman' concept never truely disappears but comes again and again haunting Nathanael. Why is advocate Coppelius or the weather glass hawker Coppola associated with 'evil principle'? Is there something supernatural at work and did Coppelius really kill his father? Nathanael insults his soon-to-be wife Clara as 'lifeless automaton' as she gives a rational explanation on his fears. But what about Spalanzani's daughter Olimpi</span>
  <span id="freeText10349982742618000375" style="display:none">Brilliant book starting with a kid's fear of the sandman. Problem is the 'sandman' concept never truely disappears but comes again and again haunting Nathanael. Why is advocate Coppelius or the weather glass hawker Coppola associated with 'evil principle'? Is there something supernatural at work and did Coppelius really kill his father? Nathanael insults his soon-to-be wife Clara as 'lifeless automaton' as she gives a rational explanation on his fears. But what about Spalanzani's daughter Olimpia Nathanael falls in love with? Olimpia is an extremely eerie character. Her and Coppelius render this tale a gothic masterpiece. Reading through the chapters you'll constantly ask yourself: how is this story going to end. Is there a happy end for Nathanael? Coppelius is one of the uncanniest and evil character you'll ever come across. He will remain with you as the 'Sandman'. Georgous book, absolutely recommended, a true classic!</span>
  <a data-text-id="10349982742618000375" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer12474915605524148662">“There is no Sandman, dear child,” replied my mother. “When I say the Sandman's coming, I only mean that you're sleepy and can't keep your eyes open – just as if sand had been sprinkled into them.”<br/>There is something very special in the tales of <b>E.T.A. Hoffmann</b> – two centuries have gone but they still remain enigmatic, startling and morosely nocturnal. <br/>“And now Nathaniel saw that a pair of eyes lay upon the ground, staring at him; these Spalanzani caught up, with his unwounded hand, and flung int</span>
  <span id="freeText12474915605524148662" style="display:none">“There is no Sandman, dear child,” replied my mother. “When I say the Sandman's coming, I only mean that you're sleepy and can't keep your eyes open – just as if sand had been sprinkled into them.”<br />There is something very special in the tales of <b>E.T.A. Hoffmann</b> – two centuries have gone but they still remain enigmatic, startling and morosely nocturnal. <br />“And now Nathaniel saw that a pair of eyes lay upon the ground, staring at him; these Spalanzani caught up, with his unwounded hand, and flung into his bosom. Then madness seized Nathaniel in its burning claws, and clutched his very soul, destroying his every sense and thought.” <br />And his tales are gloomily poetic.<br />“The painter turned round to us, but immediately proceeded with his work, saying in an indistinct, and almost inaudible voice: ‘Great deal of trouble – crooked, confused stuff – no rule to make use of – beasts – apes – human faces – human faces – miserable fool that I am!’ <br />These last words he cried aloud in a voice, that nothing but the deepest agony working in the soul could produce. I felt strangely affected; – these words, the expression of face, the glance which he had previously cast at the professor, brought before my eyes the whole struggling life of an unfortunate artist. The man could have been scarcely more than forty years old; his form, though disfigured by the unseemly, dirty costume of a painter, had something in it indescribably noble, and deep grief could only discolour his face, but could not extinguish the fire that sparkled in his black eyes.”<br />And for those who read <b>E.T.A. Hoffmann</b>’s timeless stories Sandman exists.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12474915605524148662" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Manybooks" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2970379-manybooks"><img alt="Manybooks" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459773375p2/2970379.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/78746239?book_show_action=true">Nov 23, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manybooks" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manybooks" href="/user/show/2970379-manybooks">Manybooks</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=german-literature">german-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=fairy-tales-fantasy">fairy-tales-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=folklore-kunstmarchen">folklore-kunstmarchen</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=horror">horror</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer78746239" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1730364136318343940">I read this rather (no very) creepy tale years ago, and while I truly enjoyed and above all appreciated E. T. A. Hoffmann's <u>Der Sandmann</u>, I also do not much feel like a detailed and intense rereading at this time, as the plot, the thematics actually repeatedly produced some rather vivid and glaring nightmares when I perused it, first for a German Romanticism course in undergrad and then later for my PhD Comprehensive Examinations (I still recall that there were dancing mechnical maniacal dolls, </span>
  <span id="freeText1730364136318343940" style="display:none">I read this rather (no very) creepy tale years ago, and while I truly enjoyed and above all appreciated E. T. A. Hoffmann's <u>Der Sandmann</u>, I also do not much feel like a detailed and intense rereading at this time, as the plot, the thematics actually repeatedly produced some rather vivid and glaring nightmares when I perused it, first for a German Romanticism course in undergrad and then later for my PhD Comprehensive Examinations (I still recall that there were dancing mechnical maniacal dolls, and a sandman who was anything but benevolent and was forever watching me with strange eyes, monitoring me, and during my PhD Comprehensive preparation phase, even nastily admonishing me to read my massive and awe-inspiring reading list always and ever faster and faster, yikes, it still gives me the shivers). A most definitely imaginative and wonderful, but also more than disturbing sojourn into E.T.A. Hoffmann's dark, grasping and uncanny night of the soul (interesting, even fun at times, but <u>Der Sandmann</u> is basically a story that is for all intents and purposes presents a 19th century horror genre experiment), and is thus most definitely a fairy tale (a Kunstmärchen) for adults, and NOT really appropriate for young children. And by the way, the "dancing doll" episode in Jacques Offenbach's famous opera, <i>The Tales of Hoffmann</i>, while brilliant and evocative in and of itself, is also (at least from a creepiness and uncanniness point of view) but a pale and tame reflection of <u>Der Sandmann</u> (on which the episode is distinctly but still rather loosely based).</span>
  <a data-text-id="1730364136318343940" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3609453931" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7952097571515784326">I read this wonderful story (novella) in a collection of stories/novellas titled, “Five Great German Short Stories” (edited and translated by Stanley Appelbaum). I wrote my review of each of the five stories for that collection, but wanted to add a brief comment regarding Hoffmann’s work here…<br/>I will try and refrain from giving away the plot or the story line because that’s a good chunk of the fun of reading, eh? What I did was write down some notes for each of them, and the following is a smatte</span>
  <span id="freeText7952097571515784326" style="display:none">I read this wonderful story (novella) in a collection of stories/novellas titled, “Five Great German Short Stories” (edited and translated by Stanley Appelbaum). I wrote my review of each of the five stories for that collection, but wanted to add a brief comment regarding Hoffmann’s work here…<br />I will try and refrain from giving away the plot or the story line because that’s a good chunk of the fun of reading, eh? What I did was write down some notes for each of them, and the following is a smattering of comments…there were many more that I made…I just don’t want to give stuff away…<br />•	Amazing story. <br />•	Nathaniel actually sees The Sandman who is a horrifying monster.<br />•	Coppelius a lawyer is involved with Nathaniel’s father in alchemy.<br />•	Then there’s Olimpian, an automaton created by Professor Spalanzani with help of Coppelius, and Nathaniel falls in love with her although all she says is “Ah, ah, ah”. <br />•	Young men are advised before they take a woman as a wife to ask her at least several questions so that they are convinced she’s not an automaton.<br /><br />I guess I would have to say this was one of the best short stories I have ever read. 😊 It has it all. Horror. Comedy. Macabre. Disasters. And the format of the story is so, so cool. It starts off with letters between Nathaniel and a friend Lothar but Lothar’s sister who is in love with Nathaniel reads one of them and knows Nathaniel loves her…and then the story eventually transitions to an unknown person who tells the “gentle reader” what happens to Nathaniel and others…<br /></span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13649771511937228123">Yes, people do write them - a lot of people in fact! - but the short story market today is virtually entirely for magazines. Ones like the New Yorker </span>
  <span id="freeText13649771511937228123" style="display:none">Yes, people do write them - a lot of people in fact! - but the short story market today is virtually entirely for magazines. Ones like the New Yorker or The Atlantic, which have an educated readership, or online literary/story magazines. Places were readers who are interested in "art and culture" would be willing to spend money. <br /><br />If you look on the imprint page of story collections, you'll see that many of the stories in the collection first appeared in magazines over a range of years. That's why. <br /><br />I'm referring mostly to the mass of casual readers who do not (normally) see themselves as appreciators of "art". They want a good story, period, not an artistic statement. It's hard to convince general readers that the short story is (largely) not that difficult stuff anymore, but received knowledge dies hard. 😞</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer18422541048551422151"><i>“If, like a bold painter, you had first sketched in a few audacious strokes the outline of the picture you had in your own soul, you would then easily have been able to deepen and intensify the colors one after the other, until the varied throng of living figures carried your friends away and they, like you, saw themselves in the midst of the scene that had proceeded out of your own soul.”</i><br/><br/>We had to read <i>The Sandman</i> for our literature seminar.   <span id="freeText18422541048551422151" style="display:none"><i>“If, like a bold painter, you had first sketched in a few audacious strokes the outline of the picture you had in your own soul, you would then easily have been able to deepen and intensify the colors one after the other, until the varied throng of living figures carried your friends away and they, like you, saw themselves in the midst of the scene that had proceeded out of your own soul.”</i><br /><br />We had to read <i>The Sandman</i> for our literature seminar. I was looking forward to it. I had never read anything by E.T.A. Hoffmann before and had heard great things about him.<br /><br />Personally, I couldn't connect with the story. I loved the dark and psychological images it drew. The game it played, with childhood fears and madness. It is a concept with so much potential.<br />I think it is the way this story is written that compromises my passion and liking for it. The sentences are overly long. You could make 5 individual and complex sentences out of a single one, still. Beautiful prose doesn't have to be complicated. Simplicity has a beauty of it's own.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.instagram.com/fantasticbooksandwheretofindem/" rel="nofollow noopener">Find more of my books on Instagram</a></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16813793761548528078">I felt sick to my stomach throughout most of this. There’s nothing overly supernatural, creepy, or terrifying - just a horribly unsettling undercurrent of tension and dread running underneath each word. It’s a marvel.<br/><br/>Hoffman’s skill here isn’t in depicting ghouls or demons, but in showing us the effects an encounter with such (whether perceived or otherwise) can have on the human psyche. Our protagonist is plagued by a situation from his childhood, and subconsciously seems to seek this out in hi</span>
  <span id="freeText16813793761548528078" style="display:none">I felt sick to my stomach throughout most of this. There’s nothing overly supernatural, creepy, or terrifying - just a horribly unsettling undercurrent of tension and dread running underneath each word. It’s a marvel.<br /><br />Hoffman’s skill here isn’t in depicting ghouls or demons, but in showing us the effects an encounter with such (whether perceived or otherwise) can have on the human psyche. Our protagonist is plagued by a situation from his childhood, and subconsciously seems to seek this out in his later years, leading to his descent into madness.<br /><br />I enjoyed that Hoffman left everything open to interpretation - were these ghastly villains really what our protagonist thought they were, or did he merely conjure it all in his imagination, having nested there for years as a repressed fear? Either way, it’s a heart-stopping thought.</span>

<div id="review_2751257884" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2751257884" />
    <a title="Martin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18709452-martin"><img alt="Martin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1525882833p2/18709452.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2751257884?book_show_action=true">Mar 15, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Martin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Martin" href="/user/show/18709452-martin">Martin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2751257884" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10528682998604587173">This famous German classic is 200 years old. And yet, with the current research interest in robotics and AI, it's more up to date than ever before.<br/><br/>The events of this short story are up to numerous interpretations - which seem to be a favorite pastime of those studying German literature - but basically 'The Sand Man' is the chronology of a mental illness.<br/><br/>A young college student named Nathanael recalls a traumatic childhood experience after running into a man who reminds him of the person who caus</span>
  <span id="freeText10528682998604587173" style="display:none">This famous German classic is 200 years old. And yet, with the current research interest in robotics and AI, it's more up to date than ever before.<br><br>The events of this short story are up to numerous interpretations - which seem to be a favorite pastime of those studying German literature - but basically 'The Sand Man' is the chronology of a mental illness.<br><br>A young college student named Nathanael recalls a traumatic childhood experience after running into a man who reminds him of the person who caused his father's death and who haunted his childhood as a mysterious figure named 'The Sand Man' who steals children's eyes if they don't go to bed in time. <br><br>As an adult, Nathanael is aware that the tale of the Sand Man was just that. A tale his mother had made up to get Nathanael and his siblings to go to bed whenever the creepy advocate Coppelius came to visit Nathanael's father to conduct questionable alchemical experiments with him. <br>Experiments which, eventually, cost his father's life.<br><br>His girlfriend Clara tries to talk some sense into him, reminding him that things that disturb our souls are within ourselves, so mistaking a stranger for the man who haunted his childhood doesn't make the stranger himself a threat, as long as Nathanael doesn't allow his fear to take over his life.<br><br>Nathanael's mental constitution deteriorates further when he moves into a new apartment and sees a beautiful girl through the window, sitting silently in the apartment across the street.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1552666239i/27210480.jpg" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>He falls madly in love with the silent neighbor who is said to be the daughter of a professor in the local college. <br><br>When said professor invites the town to a ball to introduce his daughter, Nathanael makes the acquaintance of Olimpia, who simply smiles and comments everything he says with 'Oh'. <br><br>In his increasing madness, Nathanael takes her simple answers as a sign of true depth, so unlike his girlfriend Clara who always seems bored when he reads his weird and dark poetry to her.<br><br>The townsfolk comment on Olimpia's strangeness, her cold skin and dead lifeless eyes. But Nathanael is determined to marry Olimpia despite his friends' warnings. <br><br>When he arrives at the professor's apartment with an engagement ring, he witnesses the professor and a man fighting over Olimpia, tearing her (mechanical robot) body apart.<br><br>This is literally the last straw for Nathanael to fall into the abyss of madness....<br><br>It's one of the coolest stories of that era that you can find.<br><br>Humanlike robots, so called 'automats' were pretty popular back in the early 19th century, so it's no wonder this theme was used so perfectly in this story. Moving machines were considered alive by people who had no idea of engineering. So having Nathanael find himself unable to distinguish between what's a real human being and what's a machine seems to be a stroke of genius to me.<br><br>I genuinely like this story and I'm so glad I've read it again after almost 20 years (when my teacher made me hold a presentation on that book in my German lessons, LOL)<br><br><b>5 stars!</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="10528682998604587173" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_167530050" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/167530050" />
    <a title=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books"><img alt=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1403239075p2/1570669.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/167530050?book_show_action=true">May 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)" class="user" itemprop="url" name=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)" href="/user/show/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books"> Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781447405818" href="/book/show/11328979-the-sandman">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">classic horror/dark fantasy readers</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=tales-to-chill-your-blood">tales-to-chill-your-blood</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=2011-reading">2011-reading</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=psychological-horror">psychological-horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=robot-cyborg-artificial-enhanced-hu">robot-cyborg-artificial-enhanced-hu</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=online-read">online-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=classic-horror">classic-horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=epistolary-narrative">epistolary-narrative</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=short-story">short-story</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer167530050" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10589163135939403375">The antiquated language and over-wrought prose on offer here will likely turn off some readers. I don’t necessarily prefer this sort of writing, and I don’t care to read it terribly often (despite my enchantment with classic horror and speculative fiction). However, part of me enjoys these elements at the same time as finding them hard to tolerate when I am not in the mood for them. Rather a contradiction, but there you have it. There’s something about the way this taste of antiquity takes me ba</span>
  <span id="freeText10589163135939403375" style="display:none">The antiquated language and over-wrought prose on offer here will likely turn off some readers. I don’t necessarily prefer this sort of writing, and I don’t care to read it terribly often (despite my enchantment with classic horror and speculative fiction). However, part of me enjoys these elements at the same time as finding them hard to tolerate when I am not in the mood for them. Rather a contradiction, but there you have it. There’s something about the way this taste of antiquity takes me back in history, so that I can experience life as those who lived during these times experienced it.<br />Nathanael is a broken man. The horrific events of his past have destroyed him in the most fundamental of ways. He is not free to be a happy, joy-filled man, content with the love of a good woman and the friendships of those around him. He is haunted by the dark memories and the malevolent figure of Coppelius, who murdered his father. This figure became intertwined in the imaginings of a childhood dark fable about the Sandman, who will punish bad children who don’t go to bed in a timely fashion.<br /><br />Years later, the memory of that diabolical man taints everything, even his relationship with Clara, his beloved. Nathanael goes back to University and starts falling in love with Olimpia, the daughter of his physics professor. She is literally the perfect woman: an exquisitely correct danger, a pianist without flaw, and beautifully mannered. She listens carefully to everything he says, not dismissing him as Clara does when he goes off on a melancholy bent. He adores and is obsessed with her, even though his friends and acquaintances find her repellent in her lack of animation. Unfortunately, his beloved is not as she seems.<br /><br />The Sandman is a study in psychological horror. Like many good horror stories, this one is laced with ambiguity. Is the malevolent figure continuing to haunt Nathanael, or has he lost his sanity, stricken by hallucinations and mental malaise; his mind broken by those terrifying events in his childhood? I wasn’t quite sure because there was evidence to suggest that it was not completely a figment of Nathanael’s imagination.<br /><br />The Sandman is an important short story because it is one of the first works of fiction to include an artificial human, the precursor to the robot of later fiction and scientific reality today. I have wanted to read this story for a while, and I enjoyed it more than not. Despite the author’s penchant for using five words when one would suffice, and the somewhat disjointed narrative structure, I found myself becoming very enthralled as I read this story. Mr. Hoffman shows a vivid imagination, and his prose caused me to become involved in the story to a level in which I was quite worried about how the story would conclude. Although this won’t be to all tastes, I recommend that admirers of classic fantasy and horrors read this one at least one, because it does have something of merit to offer to the literary world.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10589163135939403375" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText10899555647222031183" style="display:none">I have never read any of ETA Hoffmann and this gothic German horror tale is a good introduction. <div id="review_1401439928" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1401439928" />
    <a title="Joseph" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5860412-joseph"><img alt="Joseph" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1549200708p2/5860412.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1401439928?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joseph" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joseph" href="/user/show/5860412-joseph">Joseph</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847493279" href="/book/show/18778195-the-sandman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5860412-joseph?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5860412-joseph?shelf=gothic-and-supernatural">gothic-and-supernatural</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5860412-joseph?shelf=weird-fiction">weird-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5860412-joseph?shelf=fiction-in-translation">fiction-in-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5860412-joseph?shelf=novellas">novellas</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1401439928" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8702031627271261230">Nathanael’s childhood is haunted by the mysterious figure of Coppelius, a lawyer-friend of his father who regularly turns up at their house for night-time alchemical sessions. Nathanael associates Coppelius with the mythical Sandman, the legendary being said to steal the eyes of children who refuse to go to sleep. When Nathanael’s father dies as a result of an experiment gone wrong, this ominous mental link is sealed once and for all. Years later, with Nathanael now a university student, unwelco</span>
  <span id="freeText8702031627271261230" style="display:none">Nathanael’s childhood is haunted by the mysterious figure of Coppelius, a lawyer-friend of his father who regularly turns up at their house for night-time alchemical sessions. Nathanael associates Coppelius with the mythical Sandman, the legendary being said to steal the eyes of children who refuse to go to sleep. When Nathanael’s father dies as a result of an experiment gone wrong, this ominous mental link is sealed once and for all. Years later, with Nathanael now a university student, unwelcome memories are reawakened by the arrival in town of Italian barometer salesman Coppola. Could he be Coppelius under an assumed name? And what is his association with Professor Spalanzani? Nathanael’s ruminations increasingly skirt obsession. His infatuation with Olimpia, Spalanzani’s perfectly-formed but strangely uncommunicative daughter, only adds to his emotional confusion. <br /><br />I have the impression that continental Gothic tends to be more earnest and intense than English Gothic. No doubt this is a generalisation which invites any number of exceptions. However, it is certainly true of E.T.A. Hoffmann’s The Sandman (1816). Almost unremittingly dark, its bleakness is only tempered, if at all, by occasional bursts of black humour. The plot is underpinned by vague aura of supernatural dread, although from the start there is a strong suggestion that this is a tale not of ghosts or monsters but of a very human madness which may be more terrifying than any phantom.<br /><br />A classic of its kind, The Sandman has inspired later authors including Poe and composers such as Delibes and Offenbach. This annotated Alma Classics edition features a new translation by Christopher Moncrieff and includes in an Appendix a few pages from Sigmund Freud’s The Uncanny – a celebrated essay which gives a predictably psycho-sexual interpretation of Hoffmann’s novella.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8702031627271261230" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2314802557" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2314802557" />
    <a title="Kathrin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7359792-kathrin"><img alt="Kathrin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587891960p2/7359792.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2314802557?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathrin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathrin" href="/user/show/7359792-kathrin">Kathrin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9783150002308" href="/book/show/648337.Der_Sandmann">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7359792-kathrin?shelf=germany">germany</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7359792-kathrin?shelf=short-stories-novellas">short-stories-novellas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7359792-kathrin?shelf=19th-century">19th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7359792-kathrin?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7359792-kathrin?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2314802557" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3561752721924637235">Such a short but brilliant book. I remember hating it when I was forced to read it back in school but somehow ended up loving it even back then- I choose to write my final examination about its characters. <br/><br/>Rereading it, I'm still amazed by Nathanael's story. Although the book is rather short, it offers a lot. Beginning with the retelling of a horrible incident in his childhood, it proceeds to show how this early incident shapes his years as a student. Nathanael is the prime example of a youth in</span>
  <span id="freeText3561752721924637235" style="display:none">Such a short but brilliant book. I remember hating it when I was forced to read it back in school but somehow ended up loving it even back then- I choose to write my final examination about its characters. <br /><br />Rereading it, I'm still amazed by Nathanael's story. Although the book is rather short, it offers a lot. Beginning with the retelling of a horrible incident in his childhood, it proceeds to show how this early incident shapes his years as a student. Nathanael is the prime example of a youth in his time. Smart and cultivated, with a suitable marriage in his future. On the downside, he seems to be too sensitive. He can't shake the past's happenings and continues to be deeply disturbed by them. One reason may be that he isn't allowed to talk about them. He tries to write about his feelings in a letter but his fears get easily dismissed by friends and family. I feel sorry for him. <br /><br />Being a little older now, I can also appreciate the horror aspects of the story a lot more. My 16-year-old-self was disappointed because I just wasn't scared by the story. I've always been a fan of the horror genre - always seeking a book to be spooked by but to be honest, this isn't the purpose of this Kunstmärchen (a literary fairy tale?). Nevertheless, it starts to scare you if you continue thinking about it. There is something creepy about an automaton that seems to be the perfect woman. A person that is, nevertheless, described as dull and mechanical. A young man who can't tell reality from imitation. I also imagine that it was scary to think about artificial humans back when the book was first published in the early 19th century. <br /><br />I'm a big fan of the book - the only downside being its shortness - and plan on reading more by E.T.A. Hoffmann soon.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3561752721924637235" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2257033288" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16274905878315086858">... what did I just read?!<br/><br/>This is so many kinds of disturbing that nightmares are inevitable. How ironic.<br/><br/><i>
  <b>I freaking loved it,</b>
</i> by the way.<br/><br/>For a short story, there's several layers of creepy, and there's no black and white so part of its genius is in how many questions you're left with at the end.<br/><br/>I want to know <b>MORE.</b><br/><br/>I mean, surely we've all heard of The Sandman, right? The creep who throws dust in children's faces to get them to sleep? <br/><br/>Well,this delightful little short story, along with my fas</span>
  <span id="freeText16274905878315086858" style="display:none">... what did I just read?!<br /><br />This is so many kinds of disturbing that nightmares are inevitable. How ironic.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>I freaking loved it,</b>
</i> by the way.<br /><br />For a short story, there's several layers of creepy, and there's no black and white so part of its genius is in how many questions you're left with at the end.<br /><br />I want to know <b>MORE.</b><br /><br />I mean, surely we've all heard of The Sandman, right? The creep who throws dust in children's faces to get them to sleep? <br /><br />Well,this delightful little short story, along with my fascination with myths and legends, got me Googling.<br /><br />Turns out one of the earlier German interpretations of this fellow had him torturing and mutilating the kids that fought his magic sleep dust. Charming fellow. Apparently, that was inspirational stuff for Hoffman, who turned this brute into the horrifying, eye-stealing monster described in this novel.<br /><br />Anyhoo, the basic notion of the legend is that it represents how parents feel about their kids. I think there's a similar idea explored in the literary genius that is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11345309.Go_The_F__K_To_Sleep" title="Go The F**K To Sleep by Adam Mansbach" rel="nofollow noopener">Go The F**K To Sleep</a>.<br /><br />All that's fun and games, but the titular Sandman has overall a rather brief appearance, and is more of a symbol than a character. This story has way more horror beyond our sadistic, nocturnal friend.<br /><br />So basically if you love horror, read it. If you love untangling metaphors and symbols, read it. If you love myths and legends ... this is but a small glimpse. If you love short stories that deliver chills almost immediately, read it. If you want to sleep an undisturbed sleep ... read at your own risk.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16274905878315086858" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9161832222018432307">Original review posted <a href='http://thelostentwife.net/2012/02/04/the-sandman-by-e-t-a-hoffman/' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a><br/><br/>There is absolutely no reason for people to avoid reading this story. First of all, it’s short – about 30 pages (and the ones I read were TINY pages). Secondly, it’s so. freaking. amazing. Seriously! Read it! Now I’ll tell you why.<br/><br/>For my Seminar in European Literature this semester we are studying the “uncanny” – what the word means, how to define it ourselves, how it’s defined in stories, and we’re reading all sorts of fantastic things like Grimm’s Fairy Tales, Freud’</span>
  <span id="freeText9161832222018432307" style="display:none">Original review posted <a href="http://thelostentwife.net/2012/02/04/the-sandman-by-e-t-a-hoffman/" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a><br /><br />There is absolutely no reason for people to avoid reading this story. First of all, it’s short – about 30 pages (and the ones I read were TINY pages). Secondly, it’s so. freaking. amazing. Seriously! Read it! Now I’ll tell you why.<br /><br />For my Seminar in European Literature this semester we are studying the “uncanny” – what the word means, how to define it ourselves, how it’s defined in stories, and we’re reading all sorts of fantastic things like Grimm’s Fairy Tales, Freud’s essays, and other various fantastic, “uncanny” stories. Our class this semester started out with a bang – namely The Sandman by E.T.A. Hoffman. You are familiar with Hoffman, although you might not realize it. That famous Nutcracker story that graces the ballet stage every Christmas? Yup. He wrote that story.<br /><br />So now that you’ve sat back and said “Oh! That guy!”, let me introduce you to the wonders of The Sandman. When you read this story (and you will, won’t you?) I want you to think about the following things:<br /><br />What is the reality in the book?<br /><br />Just how creepy are dolls??<br /><br />What about the story really made your hair stand on end?<br /><br />Written in epistolary style, The Sandman grows in intensity, the story taking strange twists and turns until coming to an amazing, horrifying climax. I seriously shivered typing that while I recalled it. I wish there was a way to make my words literally compel you to shift away from this review and go find a copy, but there isn’t… or is there? Go read it! </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17529977764280204568">About the title story only...<br/><br/>E.T.A. HOFFMANN, The Sandman<br/>(1816). <br/><br/>Remarkably modern-feeling in theme, probably because lately we've had quite a few writers harking back to this kind of story. The sinister traveling merchant Coppelius/Coppola, selling his 'eyes-a' is reflected in “Ilse, Who Saw Clearly” by E. Lily Yu, for example. And of course, the whole steampunk genre loves to explore the idea of clockwork automata.<br/>To a modern reader, the structure of the story flows a bit oddly and unevenly, a</span>
  <span id="freeText17529977764280204568" style="display:none">About the title story only...<br /><br />E.T.A. HOFFMANN, The Sandman<br />(1816). <br /><br />Remarkably modern-feeling in theme, probably because lately we've had quite a few writers harking back to this kind of story. The sinister traveling merchant Coppelius/Coppola, selling his 'eyes-a' is reflected in “Ilse, Who Saw Clearly” by E. Lily Yu, for example. And of course, the whole steampunk genre loves to explore the idea of clockwork automata.<br />To a modern reader, the structure of the story flows a bit oddly and unevenly, and the language is quite overwrought (although this may be an artifact of translation [?]) - but its careful ambiguity and depiction of a decline into madness are effectively done. I was familiar with the plotline of the ballet, Coppélia, which was based on this story - but the original tale is far, far darker.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1360014460" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3491874240501405737">If you were to read only one story by E.T.A. Hoffman this might be it. The Sandman is one of the three tales to figure in Jacques Offenbach great opera and presents in a very forceful fashion several of the great themes that dominate Hoffman's work.<br/><br/>In this work, Hoffman poses third great questions. The first is what is the effect of childhood fears on our later lives. The second one is why do some individuals pursue illusions when better options in the real world are at hand. The last question i</span>
  <span id="freeText3491874240501405737" style="display:none">If you were to read only one story by E.T.A. Hoffman this might be it. The Sandman is one of the three tales to figure in Jacques Offenbach great opera and presents in a very forceful fashion several of the great themes that dominate Hoffman's work.<br /><br />In this work, Hoffman poses third great questions. The first is what is the effect of childhood fears on our later lives. The second one is why do some individuals pursue illusions when better options in the real world are at hand. The last question is why do people so continue on a path leading to disaster when the signs of the danger are so clear.<br /><br />Reading a single Hoffman story would be pointless. One needs to read a collection in order to thoroughly understand and enjoy the art of E.T.A. Hoffman. If the collection that you read did not include the Sandman, then I urge you to download this work which is very strong on its own and will most certainly help you to better enjoy those tales that you have already read.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2339125134" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2339125134" />
    <a title="Mina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1843780-mina"><img alt="Mina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565539108p2/1843780.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2339125134?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mina" href="/user/show/1843780-mina">Mina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook " href="/book/show/21796213-the-sand-man">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1843780-mina?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1843780-mina?shelf=novellas">novellas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1843780-mina?shelf=germany">germany</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1843780-mina?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1843780-mina?shelf=romanticism">romanticism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1843780-mina?shelf=dark">dark</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2339125134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9189822812081247884">The Sandman has a bit for everyone, although it’s the dark chocolate pack, not your average Easter basket.<br/><br/>One side of it is that Hoffmann writes tales for adults. Many of us learn to let go of the monsters that would surely come to punish us should we be naughty. Nowhere is the transition as clear as it is here, as Hoffmann builds upon the granny scaring young Nathanael with the story of the Sandman and builds him into a monster that feeds on narcissism, obsession and vanity, when Nathanael beco</span>
  <span id="freeText9189822812081247884" style="display:none">The Sandman has a bit for everyone, although it’s the dark chocolate pack, not your average Easter basket.<br /><br />One side of it is that Hoffmann writes tales for adults. Many of us learn to let go of the monsters that would surely come to punish us should we be naughty. Nowhere is the transition as clear as it is here, as Hoffmann builds upon the granny scaring young Nathanael with the story of the Sandman and builds him into a monster that feeds on narcissism, obsession and vanity, when Nathanael becomes an adult.<br /><br />There is also a technical side to Hoffman’s writing that I can’t help but enjoy. There is ample use of literary parallelism, foreshadowing, metaphore and motifs. The descriptions are on point and the plot is sharp and fast-paced, considering.<br /><br />There is a psychological layer to it, as the mephistophelian villain seems to have no effect on bystanders, and disappears as if he was never there after the first attack.<br /><br />Finally, there is ample use of irony. Near the end, when the farce is revealed, Hoffmann overlaps the unreal with the pretentious to mock the society’s love of appearances and its claims of enlightened attitudes.<br /><br />I would recommend it to anyone who enjoyed Bulgakov’s <i>The Master and Margarita,</i> and it is shorter and targets is themes concisely.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9189822812081247884" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1374980718" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2487262863198612718">I really enjoyed this. I've never read anything by E.T.A. Hoffmann before (the Sandman is part of my assigned reading for a class I'm taking this semester) but it was a really good read. Reminded me of Frankenstein in a lot of places (Nathanael's actions and letters basically screamed Victor Frankenstein to me as well as the origin of him and Clara's relationship) especially with the whole 'how far can we go with science and technology in terms of humanity' thing that Olimpia represented. The am</span>
  <span id="freeText2487262863198612718" style="display:none">I really enjoyed this. I've never read anything by E.T.A. Hoffmann before (the Sandman is part of my assigned reading for a class I'm taking this semester) but it was a really good read. Reminded me of Frankenstein in a lot of places (Nathanael's actions and letters basically screamed Victor Frankenstein to me as well as the origin of him and Clara's relationship) especially with the whole 'how far can we go with science and technology in terms of humanity' thing that Olimpia represented. The ambiguity in the story was what really stuck with me- was Nathanael mad? Was the Sandman actually real? The decline into madness was also interesting; I kept seeing the repetitive things Nathanael would say in the last page or two and his almost mechanical and involuntary actions and being reminded of Olimpia's 'words' that never changed. I felt like he almost became a machination himself. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2487262863198612718" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer5753963406716563481">4,5*<br/><br/>If you, like me, have grown up watching <i>Das Sandmännchen</i> before going to bed, you know that this story hits wayyy different - but good different. Yes, the old-fashioned syntax may be a bit difficult to get into at first if you don't read a lot of 19th century German, but I love how modern the effect of horror is that Hoffmann achieves. I wish Nathaniel's family background and relationship with Clara and Lothar had been developed a bit more, because as it stands they're rather one-dimensional</span>
  <span id="freeText5753963406716563481" style="display:none">4,5*<br /><br />If you, like me, have grown up watching <i>Das Sandmännchen</i> before going to bed, you know that this story hits wayyy different - but good different. Yes, the old-fashioned syntax may be a bit difficult to get into at first if you don't read a lot of 19th century German, but I love how modern the effect of horror is that Hoffmann achieves. I wish Nathaniel's family background and relationship with Clara and Lothar had been developed a bit more, because as it stands they're rather one-dimensional and the sister that's mentioned in Nathaniel's account of Coppelius disappears off the stage completely later on. I really liked the horror elements of this, though.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780241251508" href="/book/show/28584403-the-sandman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9429655-anna-luce?shelf=novellas-and-short-stories">novellas-and-short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9429655-anna-luce?shelf=ma-comparative-lit">ma-comparative-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9429655-anna-luce?shelf=2020-reviews">2020-reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3590705845" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7842235139020813798">Perhaps the translation is to blame but I find this to be a rather banal short story. The narrative definitely plays around with notions of the 'uncanny' but ultimately it seems to me a somewhat old-fashioned Gothic story (we have an unmanned narrator 'presenting' us with letters documenting the 'strange' events they are about to relate). I didn't like anything about this story. We have descriptions such as 'child-like child'? and a character who may or may not be Italian who says 'eyes-a'. Ther</span>
  <span id="freeText7842235139020813798" style="display:none">Perhaps the translation is to blame but I find this to be a rather banal short story. The narrative definitely plays around with notions of the 'uncanny' but ultimately it seems to me a somewhat old-fashioned Gothic story (we have an unmanned narrator 'presenting' us with letters documenting the 'strange' events they are about to relate). I didn't like anything about this story. We have descriptions such as 'child-like child'? and a character who may or may not be Italian who says 'eyes-a'. There was something about the tone of this story that brought to mind <i>Frankenstein</i>, so perhaps if you, unlike me, enjoyed that classic you might find 'The Sandman' to be a diverting read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7842235139020813798" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4519053232587305130">I had my eyes on it for a while (my plan was to read in German back when I had to read some German novels; glad I didn't read it back then though, because a) I don't know that much German and b) I think it's even better in English (I can't compare it though, but I mean that I understand it now more I think than when I read it in German). KarinaE (over at Booktube) read it and ever since I wanted to read it too, so I was really excited when I found out it was going to be published in this edition</span>
  <span id="freeText4519053232587305130" style="display:none">I had my eyes on it for a while (my plan was to read in German back when I had to read some German novels; glad I didn't read it back then though, because a) I don't know that much German and b) I think it's even better in English (I can't compare it though, but I mean that I understand it now more I think than when I read it in German). KarinaE (over at Booktube) read it and ever since I wanted to read it too, so I was really excited when I found out it was going to be published in this edition.<br /> <br />This story is dark, but really good. It's about how obsession can drive you crazy; how dreams and desire can drive you crazy too. This tale begins with three letters, but then it continuos through the eyes of a writer (it's strange yes, just read it) and then he tells us what happens next. I hoped it would be a tale through the young eyes of the character, but most of the story is from his adult point of view. However, I still liked it and recommend you guys to check it out as well. </span>

  <span id="freeText8052318634503084994" style="display:none">A masterpiece of horror fiction, which features a surprisingly modern and complex dialectical analysis of the plot events by the characters themselves, who divide into rationalist and subjectivist camps and offer a kind of running commentary on the story. I'm surprised it was never subjected to a book-length deconstruction by Derrida or Barthes. <br /><br />The story is an unmistakable milestone in the formation of the modern conception of the unconscious. It tremendously impressed Freud and Kafka, is a cornerstone of intellectual history, and is a damned entertaining story to boot. A unique and compelling work.</span>

<div id="review_1460833809" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1460833809" />
    <a title="Sam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1768003-sam"><img alt="Sam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574266913p2/1768003.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1460833809?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam" href="/user/show/1768003-sam">Sam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847493279" href="/book/show/18778195-the-sandman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1768003-sam?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1768003-sam?shelf=shorts">shorts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1768003-sam?shelf=proper-books">proper-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1768003-sam?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1460833809" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17159513148918904238">This is a disturbing and haunting tale told in the classic Gothic style that leaves more to the imagination to the reader than modern stories and is all the more chilling as a result. The story starts with Nathanael recounting some of his childhood memories of Coppelius, a man who used to visit his father regularly to conduct strange alchemical experiments which were linked to his death. These memories have haunted Nathanael ever since and come back with force when an Italian and his daughter mo</span>
  <span id="freeText17159513148918904238" style="display:none">This is a disturbing and haunting tale told in the classic Gothic style that leaves more to the imagination to the reader than modern stories and is all the more chilling as a result. The story starts with Nathanael recounting some of his childhood memories of Coppelius, a man who used to visit his father regularly to conduct strange alchemical experiments which were linked to his death. These memories have haunted Nathanael ever since and come back with force when an Italian and his daughter moves to Nathanael's town. And so begins a spiral into hell or madness where reality mixes with the supernatural and/or superstition and the reader is pulled along with it. As is tradition with such tales it is never obvious whether it is all in Nathanael's head or whether the events he experiences are real, or even maybe a bit of both, which makes this all the more disturbing as it feeds into each readers' deepest fears. The writing is detailed and a little old fashioned (hardly surprising) but this adds to the feel of the story and the engrossing nature of the story, and personally I do love the older style of writing, especially for these kinds of Gothic horrors.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17159513148918904238" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('RfJNLD5/Z8baahdmwqLPGIkPJXMwlcUISbFM5AT3VIlvw47/sc45iq/+bl8JvWRg6QaCf/MsAwLfFxmETE6h/g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('JOHMIRdWF8JtfAV7eHqZFhq6ZH+C/AvvLtPNXkeUghoO0A/ymOdJjhjofEKzZTJuerPDc0FFzeW4dZg+Dy13bQ==')})
  }

Mit seinem Nathan reagierte Lessing auf die religiöse Orthodoxie und Intoleranz seiner Zeit. Jahrhundert - eine literarische Form, die mit der klassizistischen Auffassung und Tragödie bricht und Probleme des häuslich-privaten Bereichs und den Konflikt der Stände zum Gegenstand der tragischen Handlung macht. In sehr direkter Weise wird in Schillers \'Kabale und Liebe\' mit seinem auf Spannung angelegten klaren Aufbau Kritik an den politischen Zuständen in der Fürstentümern geübt: Willkür, Mätressenwirtschaft, Verkauf von Landeskindern. Das bürgerliche Trauerspiel kontrastiert die verrottete Moral des Hofes mit der Tugend des Bürgertums. Im Mittelpunkt des Sturm-und-Drang-Stücks steht die tragische Liebesbeziehung zwischen Angehörigen verschiedener Stände: zwischen dem Adligen Ferdinand und der Musikertochter Luise Miller. \'Kabale             e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('bookCover_3576977').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>

</li>
</ul>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/7267068.E_T_A_Hoffmann">About E.T.A. Hoffmann</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/7267068.E_T_A_Hoffmann"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1379382085p3/7267068.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/7267068.E_T_A_Hoffmann">E.T.A. Hoffmann
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
560
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11458024174675217720">Ernst Theodor Wilhelm Hoffmann, better known by his pen name E. T. A. Hoffmann (Ernst Theodor Amadeus Hoffmann), was a German Romantic author of fantasy and horror, a jurist, composer, music critic, draftsman and caricaturist. His stories form the basis of Jacques Offenbach's famous opera <i>The Tales of Hoffmann</i>, in which Hoffman appears (heavily fictionalized) as the hero. He is also the author of </span>
  <span id="freeText11458024174675217720" style="display:none">Ernst Theodor Wilhelm Hoffmann, better known by his pen name E. T. A. Hoffmann (Ernst Theodor Amadeus Hoffmann), was a German Romantic author of fantasy and horror, a jurist, composer, music critic, draftsman and caricaturist. His stories form the basis of Jacques Offenbach's famous opera <i>The Tales of Hoffmann</i>, in which Hoffman appears (heavily fictionalized) as the hero. He is also the author of the novella <i>The Nutcracker and the Mouse King</i>, on which the famous ballet <i>The Nutcracker</i> is based. The ballet <i>Coppélia</i> is based on two other stories that Hoffmann wrote, while Schumann's <i>Kreisleriana</i> is based on Hoffmann's character Johannes Kreisler.<br /><br />Hoffmann's stories were very influential during the 19th century, and he is one of the major authors of the Romantic movement.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11458024174675217720" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
